        <script type="text/javascript">
            function wpSolutionToggle(solution, link, showtext, hidetext) {
                if (solution.style.display != 'none') {
                    solution.style.display = 'none';
                    link.innerHTML = showtext;
                    link.className = 'solution_link_show';
                } else {
                    solution.style.display = 'block';
                    link.innerHTML = hidetext;
                    link.className = 'solution_link_hide';
                }
            }

<script type="text/javascript">
//<![CDATA[



/* JQUERY */
jQuery(document).ready(function(){ 
    
   
  

	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish(); */
	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish().find('ul').bgIframe({opacity:false}); */
 
	/* For IE6 */
	if (jQuery.browser.msie && /MSIE 6\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 7\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 8\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent)) {

		/* Max-width for images in IE6 */		
		var centerwidth = jQuery("td#middle").width(); 
		
		/* Images without caption */
		jQuery(".post img").each(function() { 
			var maxwidth = centerwidth - 10 + 'px';
			var imgwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var imgheight = jQuery(this).height(); 
			var newimgheight = (centerwidth / imgwidth * imgheight) + 'px';	
			if (imgwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxwidth}); 
				jQuery(this).css({height: newimgheight}); 
			}
		});
		
		/* Images with caption */
		jQuery("div.wp-caption").each(function() { 
			var captionwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var maxcaptionwidth = centerwidth + 'px';
			var captionheight = jQuery(this).height();
			var captionimgwidth =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").width();
			var captionimgheight =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").height();
			if (captionwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxcaptionwidth}); 
				var newcaptionheight = (centerwidth / captionwidth * captionheight) + 'px';
				var newcaptionimgheight = (centerwidth / captionimgwidth * captionimgheight) + 'px';
				jQuery(this).css({height: newcaptionheight}); 
				jQuery("div.wp-caption img").css({height: newcaptionimgheight}); 
				}
		});
		
		/* sfhover for LI:HOVER support in IE6: */
		jQuery("ul li").
			hover( function() {
					jQuery(this).addClass("sfhover")
				}, 
				function() {
					jQuery(this).removeClass("sfhover")
				} 
			); 

	/* End IE6 */
	}
	
	
	
	/* Since 3.7.8: Auto resize videos (embed and iframe elements) 
	TODO: Parse parent's dimensions only once per layout column, not per video
	*/
	function bfa_resize_video() {
		jQuery('embed, iframe').each( function() {
			var video = jQuery(this),
			videoWidth = video.attr('width'); // use the attr here, not width() or css()
			videoParent = video.parent(),
			videoParentWidth = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'width' ) ),
			videoParentBorder = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-left-width' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-right-width' ) ),
			videoParentPadding = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-left' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-right' ) ),
			maxWidth = videoParentWidth - videoParentBorder - videoParentPadding;

			if( videoWidth > maxWidth ) {
				var videoHeight = video.attr('height'),
				videoMaxHeight = ( maxWidth / videoWidth * videoHeight );
				video.attr({ width: maxWidth, height: videoMaxHeight });
			} 

		});	
	}
	bfa_resize_video();
	jQuery(window).resize( bfa_resize_video );

		
	jQuery(".post table tr").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("over");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("over");
		});

	
	jQuery(".post table tr:even").
		addClass("alt");

	
	jQuery("input.text, input.TextField, input.file, input.password, textarea").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("highlight"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("highlight"); 
		})
	
	jQuery("input.inputblur").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("inputfocus"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("inputfocus"); 
		})

		

	
	jQuery("input.button, input.Button, input#submit").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("buttonhover");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("buttonhover");
		});

	/* toggle "you can use these xhtml tags" */
	jQuery("a.xhtmltags").
		click(function(){ 
			jQuery("div.xhtml-tags").slideToggle(300); 
		});

	/* For the Tabbed Widgets plugin: */
	jQuery("ul.tw-nav-list").
		addClass("clearfix");

		
	
});

//]]>
</script>

								<div class="post-34 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-cpp-tutorial odd" id="post-34">
						<div class="post-headline">		<h1>1.12 &#8212; Chapter 1 comprehensive quiz</h1>
		</div>		<div class="post-byline">By Alex, on June 3rd, 2007</div>		<div class="post-bodycopy clearfix"><p><strong>Question 1</strong></p>
<p>Write a single-file program (named main.cpp) that reads two separate integers from the user, adds them together, and then outputs the answer.  The program should use three functions:</p>
<ul>
<li>A function named &#8220;readNumber&#8221; should be used to get (and return) a single integer from the user.</li>
<li>A function named &#8220;writeAnswer&#8221; should be used to output the answer.  This function should take a single parameter and have no return value.</li>
<li>A main() function should be used to glue the above functions together.
</ul>
<p>Hint: You do not need to write a separate function to do the adding (just use operator+ directly).<br />
Hint: You will need to call readNumber() twice.<br />
Hint: If you&#8217;re using visual studio with precompiled headers, don&#8217;t forget to #include &#8220;stdafx.h&#8221;.</p>
<p><strong>Question 2</strong></p>
<p>Modify the program you wrote in exercise #1 so that readNumber and writeAnswer() live in a separate file called &#8220;io.cpp&#8221;.  Use a forward declaration to access them from main().</p>
<p>Hint: If you&#8217;re having problems, make sure io.cpp is properly added to your project so it gets compiled (see lession <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/18-programs-with-multiple-files/">1.8 -- programs with multiple files</a> for more information on how to do this</a>).</p>
<p><strong>Question 3</strong></p>
<p>Modify the program you wrote in #2 so that it uses a header file (named io.h) to access the functions instead of forward declarations.  Make sure your header file uses header guards.</p>
<p><strong>Quiz solutions</strong></p>
<p>1) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id1042193990'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>

		<p>The linker should detect both your cpp files (main and io) and will sandwich them both together along with some other dependency libraries.</p>
<p>If you wanted to write a header file then there would be no need for a forward declaration in your Main.cpp, you would just #include &quot;io.h&quot; and the .cpp file would be sandwiched during compilation.&nbsp;&nbsp;Both ways work, but normally having a seperate .h file will allow us to avoid forward declarations in our Main, since including the header would just do that for us automagically.</p>
		</div>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-6162">
		<div id="div-comment-6162" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://www.tarindel.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Alex</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-6162">
		January 16, 2008 at 1:15 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=6162#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-6162", "6162", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hmmm.  When we say &#8220;int x&#8221; or &#8220;int y&#8221;, we&#8217;re telling the program that we&#8217;re defining a new variable, and we&#8217;re telling the program what type the variable is and what it&#8217;s name is.  When a new variable is declared inside of a function, that variable&#8217;s name is only meaningful within that function.  As a result, it is perfectly permissible to reuse the same variable name within multiple functions.  This does not imply any kind of linkage between these variables.</p>
<p>Within main(), we declare two variables: x and y.  These variables only exist within main().  We need two variables here because we have to hold two inputs.</p>
<p>Within ReadNumber(), we also declare one variable named x.  This x has no relationship to the x we declared within main().  In fact, ReadNumber() doesn&#8217;t even know that another variable with the same name exists within main().  This is a good thing because it means we don&#8217;t have to worry about naming collisions.</p>
<p>We do not declare a variable y within ReadNumber() because ReadNumber() only needs one variable to do it&#8217;s job.  Once ReadNumber() is finished executing, it passes it&#8217;s value back to the caller (main()).  Main() then assigns that value to one of the variables it declared.</p>
<p>It might help you understand conceptually if you rename the x variable inside of ReadNumber() to &#8220;z&#8221;, or some other name that is not reused by main().</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-6385">
		<div id="div-comment-6385" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://www.tarindel.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Alex</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-6385">
		January 20, 2008 at 9:11 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=6385#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-6385", "6385", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>WriteAnswer() actually does not return anything.  When the program reaches the <code>WriteAnswer(x+y)</code> line, the first thing that happens is that x+y is evaluated and resolved to an integer.  After that happens, then WriteAnswer() is called.  The value of x+y is passed as a parameter of WriteAnswer().  This is why WriteAnswer has a parameter of type &#8220;int&#8221; -- because the caller is passing it a parameter of type int.  WriteAnswer() doesn&#8217;t return anything back to the caller, which is why it has a return type of void.</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">

Throughout this tutorial, I have been observing the effects of typing in numbers, letters, and even expressions, when it is expecting an integer.  You have yet to explain what it does in these cases or how to validate proper inputs.  I usually validate inputs in my programs.  This is my first low-ish level language, usually deal with higher stuff like MATLAB.  I&#8217;m sure this all will be explained in later sections. I want to tell you what happens though.</p>
<p>I enter the letter s for the first number.  it prints OK to the screen, since there isn&#8217;t any validation, it always does.  It asks for the second number.  But it answers itself with the word OK, and prints the answer as 0. How does s + OK equal 0?  we should clear the input buffer every function then?</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-8787">
		<div id="div-comment-8787" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://www.tarindel.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Alex</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-8787">
		March 2, 2008 at 11:55 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=8787#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-8787", "8787", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Chad, I&#8217;m just starting to write the sections on I/O (chapter 13), where I will discuss I/O in more detail.  Unfortunately, there is no easy way to do numerical validation in C++ when using the standard I/O operators (at least that I know of).  As you&#8217;ve noted, the >> operator does funny things when the user input is not formatted as expected.  There are some other functions that perform more consistently, which I will cover in chapter 13.2 (which should be up sometime this week).</p>
<p>However, perhaps the best way I&#8217;ve found is to read everything into a string, because a string will treat numbers, letters, and whitespace uniformly.  Then, you can parse the string to see whether it meets your validation criteria.  However, this is somewhat beyond the scope of these introductory tutorials.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

		<p>I am a bit perplexed as to why it won&#8217;t compile for you.  I copied your code into a WriteAnswer.cpp and it worked fine for me.  Your error messages don&#8217;t make any sense for the code you copied above.  Character 0xa0 is the &#8216;&#8217; character.  You might look through your files and see if somehow one of your &#8216;a&#8217; characters is actually an &#8216;&#8217; instead.</p>
<p>As for the pausing issue, Visual Studio intercepts the program&#8217;s termination and holds the output window open until you hit a key.  This is a nice feature that it provides.  When you run your program directly from the .exe, it exits immediately upon termination because that&#8217;s what it should do under normal circumstances. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>

		<p>For a program this simple it&#8217;s probably okay, but for more complex programs using global variables is a bad idea and should generally be avoided.  I talk more about this in <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/42-global-variables/" rel="nofollow">lesson 4.2 -- Global variables</a>.</p>

		<p>Code questions about what went wrong with your program are really better suited to be posted elsewhere.</p>
<p>In this case though, the answer looks easy -- your prototyped functions in addsub.h do not match the ones you declared in the .cpp files.  The ones in the .cpp files have no parameters.</p>

		<p>This program won&#8217;t compile because you&#8217;ve got multiple definitions for both functions ReadNumber() and WriteNumber().</p>
<p>Remember that variables declared in a function are local to that function -- the x and y you&#8217;re declaring in main() are different than the ones in ReadNumber() or WriteNumber().</p>

		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-48425">
		<div id="div-comment-48425" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://www.tarindel.com' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>Alex</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-48425">
		March 1, 2009 at 11:41 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=48425#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-48425", "48425", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Putting your code in the .cpp file isn&#8217;t strictly necessary with these short little programs.  However, it IS a good idea to do it anyway because once you get farther along (especially once you get to classes) you&#8217;ll realize that putting code in the headers really clutters up your headers and makes it hard to figure out the structure of your program.</p>
<p>Furthermore, if you put code in the headers and then have to change that code, you&#8217;ll have to not only recompile that header, but everything that includes it too.  That means one little change can have a cascading effect on what needs to be recompiled, which can really slow down compilation times.</p>
<p>So even though it&#8217;s not necessary now, it&#8217;s good to get in good habits now so you don&#8217;t have to break bad habits later. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=59736#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-59736", "59736", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>

		<p>Generally it is inadvisable to include .cpp files in other .cpp files, as it&#8217;s easy for a .cpp to get included twice that way.  for example, if A.cpp includes B.cpp, B.cpp may get included once from A.cpp and again by the project itself.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

		<div id="div-comment-251444" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251444">
		February 10, 2015 at 1:04 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251444#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251444", "251444", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>There are multiple problems here:<br />
1) Inside of Read(), you&#8217;re declaring variables x and y.  These are local function Read() and get destroyed when the call to Read() is finished.<br />
2) You can&#8217;t mix a call to cout with a call to cin.<br />
3) On line 20, you&#8217;re printing the address of the function Write, not actually making a function call to Write().  Don&#8217;t forget that function calls need a parameter list, enclosed in ().</p>
		</div>

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-82052">
		<div id="div-comment-82052" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/eec5a37eafe8ab978daeeb442a785a52?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/eec5a37eafe8ab978daeeb442a785a52?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">SkinnY</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-82052">
		March 30, 2010 at 2:33 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=82052#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-82052", "82052", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to SkinnY'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>&#8220;int add(int x, int y)&#8221; is a forward declaration, which require a &#8220;;&#8221; at the end of.<br />
The error was found in line 8 because it was expecting itself to be part of a prototype function, waiting for a {, causing the compiler to complain.</p>
<p>Your code doesn&#8217;t look too bad, but you should break it down some more.</p>
		</div>

		<p>Your int WriteAnswer prototype function should be above main. //Because when WriteAnswer is called in main(), it will not know what you&#8217;re referring to yet.<br />
Also in your main function, &#8220;WriteAnswer(num1, num2)&#8221; should be &#8220;WriteAnswer(num1 + num2)&#8221;.//Without adding a symbol in there the variables will not give an argument.</p>

WriteAnswer() also doesn&#8217;t do any outputting.  It just adds two values.  In this case, WriteAnswer() would be better named add().</p>

		<p>Hey there&#8230;this is my first night on here, so beginner on c++ eh. But I thought I did everything correctly, but I am stuck with 1 error.</p>
<p>LINK : fatal error LNK1561: entry point must be defined</p>
<p>Here&#8217;s my 3 files:<br />
=============================================================================================================<br />
io.cpp:</p>
<p>#include<br />
int ReadNumber()<br />
{<br />
    using namespace std;<br />
    cout &lt;&gt; x;<br />
    return x;<br />
}</p>
<p>void WriteAnswer(int x)<br />
{<br />
    using namespace std;<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;The answer is &quot; &lt;&lt; x &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
}<br />
=============================================================================================================</p>
<p>io.h:</p>
<p>#ifndef IO_H<br />
#define IO_H</p>
<p>int ReadNumber();<br />
void WriteAnswer(int x);</p>
<p>#endif</p>
<p>=============================================================================================================</p>
<p>main.cpp:</p>
<p>#include &quot;io.h&quot;</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
    int x = ReadNumber();<br />
    int y = ReadNumber();<br />
    WriteAnswer(x+y);<br />
    return 0;<br />
}</p>

		<p>Your io.cpp needs #include  heading it, because you are calling functions that require that library.<br />
You&#8217;re not taking in a value from your int ReadNumber() function, so the compiler does not know where to start at.<br />

id="crayon-555d74058231c507878302-20"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>

		<li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix" id="comment-77101">
		<div id="div-comment-77101" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/650c7483bb6b0795ebb00af15b7a5c63?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/650c7483bb6b0795ebb00af15b7a5c63?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">jason</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-77101">
		January 23, 2010 at 8:28 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=77101#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-77101", "77101", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to jason'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Funny, everyone seemed to be stumped by the same thing I was, the fact that WriteAnswer only had one int but main had two. Before reading posts about this or continuing with the test, I went back to the section on functions and figured it out, but this was a very tricky question to put on the test, since your examples were all (a,b) in both &#8220;main&#8221; and the &#8220;add&#8221; and &#8220;multiply&#8221; functions. I think you should go back to that section and add a blurb about the relationship between main and external functions, because though it all makes sense once you figure it out, this leads to unnecessary head-scratching for us fledgling coders. You could write a pared-down version of your response in an earlier post:</p>
<p>&#8220;Hmmm. When we say int x or int y, were telling the program that were defining a new variable, and were telling the program what type the variable is and what its name is. When a new variable is declared inside of a function, that variables name is only meaningful within that function. As a result, it is perfectly permissible to reuse the same variable name within multiple functions. This does not imply any kind of linkage between these variables.</p>
<p>Within main(), we declare two variables: x and y. These variables only exist within main(). We need two variables here because we have to hold two inputs.</p>
<p>Within ReadNumber(), we also declare one variable named x. This x has no relationship to the x we declared within main(). In fact, ReadNumber() doesnt even know that another variable with the same name exists within main(). This is a good thing because it means we dont have to worry about naming collisions.</p>
<p>We do not declare a variable y within ReadNumber() because ReadNumber() only needs one variable to do its job. Once ReadNumber() is finished executing, it passes its value back to the caller (main()). Main() then assigns that value to one of the variables it declared.</p>
<p>It might help you understand conceptually if you rename the x variable inside of ReadNumber() to z, or some other name that is not reused by main().&#8221;</p>
		</div>

		<p>Since this question was posted, I&#8217;ve added a new lesson that I hopes make clear the relationship between variables in main and other functions: <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4b-a-first-look-at-local-scope/" rel="nofollow">1.4b -- a first look at local scope</a>.</p>

		<p>Hey dayana,</p>
<p>Yeah, I was breaking my head over this for a bit now (And to think I&#8217;m revising C++ here. First time at headers though).</p>
<p>What I did is this:<br />
1) Go to Start -&gt; Run -&gt; Type &#8220;cmd&#8221; (without the quotes.<br />
2) The Command Prompt should open.<br />
3) Navigate your way to where your code is.<br />
4) Enter the folder where your code is stored.<br />
5) If your program is named &#8220;BLAH.cpp&#8221;, just type BLAH and press Enter.</p>
<p>The Command Prompt seems to include your file automatically.</p>
<p>Do let me know if it works.</p>
		</div>

		<p>Writing your own code is hard, and takes practice.  I suspect many people will find this quiz challenging, because it&#8217;s asking you to synthesize knowledge that you&#8217;ve been presented over many lessons.</p>
<p>It&#8217;s okay if you are unsuccessful, so long as you follow up and understand why the provided answer works.</p>
<p>One additional good way to learn is to try and extend the program.  For example, can you make it add 3 numbers?</p>

		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-95552">

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=95552#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-95552", "95552", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to zingmars'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>It&#8217;s because once the result is printed on the screen, the program receives the command to return 0 to OS, closing the program. To prevent that there are multiple options, however the best one in this case would be adding cin &gt;&gt; x; (or anything you&#8217;ve declared before, if you&#8217;re not feeling like declaring something new) before the return 0;</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-3 clearfix" id="comment-251460">
		<div id="div-comment-251460" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251460">
		February 10, 2015 at 2:59 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251460#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251460", "251460", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>A header file is the worst place you can use a using statement, because everybody that includes that header will import the using statement as well.</p>
<p>It&#8217;s best to use the using statements in each individual function that needs them.  Or avoid them altogether and use the std:: prefix.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

		<p>Is it possible you #included io.cpp instead of .h?  If you did, you may have gotten two copies of ReadNumber() and WriteAnswer(): one when your compiler compiled io.cpp, and another when the contents of io.cpp were imported into main.cpp.</p>

						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d740580bd9303405866-1"><span class="crayon-o">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-v">code</span><span class="crayon-o">&gt;</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d740580bd9303405866-2"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">main</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">{</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d740580bd9303405866-3"><span class="crayon-h">	</span><span class="crayon-e">WriteAnswer</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-e">ReadNumber</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">+</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">ReadNumber</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d740580bd9303405866-4"><span class="crayon-h">	</span><span class="crayon-st">return</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">0</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d740580bd9303405866-5"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d740580bd9303405866-6"><span class="crayon-o">&lt;</span><span class="crayon-o">/</span><span class="crayon-v">code</span><span class="crayon-o">&gt;</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0010 seconds] -->
<p>It works for a small program like this, but obviously since I don&#8217;t store the user inputs or answer, I can&#8217;t use them later on if I needed to</p>

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=175066#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-175066", "175066", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to John Fiala'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hey Brad.  I realize you posted this a year ago, but it&#8217;s a pretty high-up comment and I had the same question.  Here&#8217;s an answer I found on stack overflow: http://stackoverflow.com/questions/895827/what-is-the-difference-between-tmain-and-main-in-c</p>
<p>In short, _tmain is a macro that the compiler has defined to deal with unicode switching.  If you have unicode off, it&#8217;s replaced by main, and if you have it on, it&#8217;s replaced by wmain, apparently.  </p>
<p>So, if you&#8217;re using Visual Studio and you get _tmain, it&#8217;s cool to leave it.</p>
		</div>

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-208368">
		<div id="div-comment-208368" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">rameye</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-208368">
		November 3, 2014 at 1:39 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=208368#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-208368", "208368", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Make sure that you have <b>io.cpp</b> included into your IDE project, or it will not be included in the build and compiled. If <b>io.cpp</b> is not compiled there will be no object file containing the <b>io.cpp</b> functions. Therefore even though you have used <b>#include &#8220;io.h&#8221;</b> in your <b>main.cpp</b>, the linker will not be able to find the compiled functions. Doing a <b>#include &#8220;io.cpp&#8221;</b> is not needed and is bad form. Only include .h header files that have the forward declaration prototypes of functions that live in the .cpp file. But it is still your responsibility to ensure that the .cpp files are all compiled when you do the build with your IDE. The bottom line is both <b>main.cpp</b> and <b>io.cpp</b> should be source files included in your IDE project. Then it will all come together correctly when you do the build.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-97807">
		<div id="div-comment-97807" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/8fbba8b033309c942e0247e39e8b8fa3?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/8fbba8b033309c942e0247e39e8b8fa3?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Frelion</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-97807">
		September 15, 2012 at 9:39 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=97807#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-97807", "97807", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Frelion'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>1. return x is in that function so it can give the number from the user &#8220;x&#8221; to be used in main. (it sends/returns the user&#8217;s number that they input back to main)<br />
2. void is used at the beginning of functions to tell main that it won&#8217;t return a number.<br />
3. writeAnswer works because the parameter inside the parentheses gets used in main once main pulls x from the readnumber() function, who told the user to enter a number, in the last answer to the quiz, you can add two numbers together in the parameter for writeAnswer and it will solve it and use the sum as the new number to be printed.<br />
4. int y was assigned to another readnumber() function, so now you have 2 of those functions and thus 2 new numbers from saved from the user.<br />
5. There is only one ReadNumber() function, but it was called twice, and using my answer from number 4, you do that to make programming easier and so you don&#8217;t have to type as much (or hard-code as much) later on.<br />
5.Also. because you called the function twice and saved the numbers as different assigned variables in main all the actions of that function happen twice so it once again asks for input.<br />
5.Also.2. only that x was declared and it was put into a different x and a y in main, it was called twice so you wouldn&#8217;t have to type as much.<br />
Wow, answering these questions clearly are hard&#8230; If you are struggling with all that stuff, I recommend that you start over the tutorials up until here until it all makes sense. You need to learn which x is tied to which and what happens if you change the name of your variables in functions vs what you call them in main.  Hope it helped, I love these tut&#8217;s, thanks!</p>
		</div>

</ul><!-- .children -->

		<li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix" id="comment-99449">
		<div id="div-comment-99449" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cac92f84b10681d0d3c6c5d54ae79cbf?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/cac92f84b10681d0d3c6c5d54ae79cbf?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">cmastah</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-99449">
		June 10, 2013 at 10:22 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=99449#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-99449", "99449", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to cmastah'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hi, thanks for the awesome website and the excellent opportunity to learn C++. I wanted to ask:</p>
<p>1. Why is it that in WriteAnswer(int x) we put the int x within brackets whereas with ReadNumber(), the int x is within the function itself. I tried putting the int x within the WriteAnswer function instead of within the brackets but got errors.</p>
<p>2. Why is it that when we declare int x and int y in the main() function, that typing them up as &#8220;int x = ReadNumber();&#8221; actually executes/compiles/starts the function rather than just take it as a declaration of int x. I would&#8217;ve thought we&#8217;d have to actually type it up as:</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
     int x;<br />
     int y;<br />
     ReadNumber(int x);<br />
     ReadNumber(int y);<br />
     WriteAnswer(x+y);<br />
}</p>
<p>When doing them as such, I got a &#8220;error: expected primary-expression before int&#8221;.</p>
<p>3. When I do the program as outlined above and instead use &#8220;ReadNumber(int)&#8221;, I get an error but I&#8217;m not sure why.</p>
<p>4. If we chose to redo WriteAnswer as &#8220;int WriteAnswer(int x)&#8221; with return 0, it still works. Is there any specific reason we&#8217;d go with void over int?</p>
<p>5. This goes a little further from this specific section, but I&#8217;m curious, what is the caller? I would&#8217;ve thought the user would be the caller. I ask because void means the function doesn&#8217;t return any values to the caller but can return values to a user like in the above function. I still feel myself stumped by void, is there any in-depth explanation I can read on void and its uses?</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-99468">
		<div id="div-comment-99468" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f2ef63735fb91ef73dab9e19f08330b3?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/f2ef63735fb91ef73dab9e19f08330b3?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">RevEng</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-99468">
		June 11, 2013 at 8:16 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=99468#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-99468", "99468", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to RevEng'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p><strong>1. Why is it that in WriteAnswer(int x) we put the int x within brackets whereas with ReadNumber(), the int x is within the function itself. I tried putting the int x within the WriteAnswer function instead of within the brackets but got errors.</strong></p>
<p><i>The &#8220;int x&#8221; allows and tells the WriteAnswer function to expect and process an integer called &#8220;x&#8221; from the calling function (in this case &#8220;main()&#8221;.  Whereas, the empty parentheses &#8220;()&#8221; tells the ReadNumber function that there is no input being passed (no incoming parameters to process) to the function.</i></p>
<p><strong>2. Why is it that when we declare int x and int y in the main() function, that typing them up as int x = ReadNumber(); actually executes/compiles/starts the function rather than just take it as a declaration of int x. I wouldve thought wed have to actually type it up as:</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
int x;<br />
int y;<br />
ReadNumber(int x);<br />
ReadNumber(int y);<br />
WriteAnswer(x+y);<br />
}</p>
<p>When doing them as such, I got a error: expected primary-expression before int.</strong></p>
<p><i> ReadNumber() returns a value that will be assigned to &#8220;x&#8221; (or &#8220;y&#8221; for the second call).  When you place &#8220;int x&#8221; inside the parentheses, you are passing the value of &#8220;x&#8221; in main() to the ReadNumber() function.  This is not valid, since the ReadNumber() function does not accept any parameters.</p>
<p>So, if you want to give a function a parameter and that function is set up to receive a parameter, put it inside the parentheses-e.g. WriteAnswer(int x).  If you are calling a function that doesn&#8217;t accept parameters, you leave the parentheses empty-e.g. ReadNumber().</p>
<p>The reason you received the error is that ReadNumber() function does not receive any parameters.  You cannot pass (int x) or (int y) to ReadNumber().  REMEMBER: When you call a function, make sure the number of parameters in your calling function matches the number of parameters the called function expects.</i></p>
<p><strong>3. When I do the program as outlined above and instead use ReadNumber(int), I get an error but Im not sure why.</strong></p>
<p><i>See answers to Questions 1 and 2.</i></p>
<p><strong>4. If we chose to redo WriteAnswer as int WriteAnswer(int x) with return 0, it still works. Is there any specific reason wed go with void over int?</strong></p>
<p><i>Whenever a called function completes, it will return a value.  The type of expected return value is stated before the function on the same line.  </p>
<p>For example:<br />
<strong>void WriteAnswer(int x)</strong> means that WriteAnswer will return &#8220;void&#8221;-in other words, nothing-when it finishes doing what it&#8217;s supposed to do.</p>
<p><strong>int ReadNumber()</strong> means that ReadNumber will return an &#8220;int&#8221; or integer when it finishes doing what it&#8217;s supposed to do.  In this case, the returned value will be the number that the user entered.</i></p>
<p><strong>5. This goes a little further from this specific section, but Im curious, what is the caller? I wouldve thought the user would be the caller. I ask because void means the function doesnt return any values to the caller but can return values to a user like in the above function. I still feel myself stumped by void, is there any in-depth explanation I can read on void and its uses?</strong></p>
<p><i>The &#8220;caller&#8221; is the name of the function that /calls/ another function.  For example: main() calls ReadNumber() and WriteAnswer(x+y)-&#8220;main&#8221; is the /caller/ and ReadNumber and WriteAnswer are the /called/ functions.  If ReadNumber calls another function, such as &#8220;cin&#8221;, then, ReadNumber is the /caller/ and &#8220;cin&#8221; is the /called/ function.  It&#8217;s just a matter of point of reference when using the word &#8220;caller&#8221; vs. &#8220;called&#8221;.</p>
<p>As for the rest of your question regarding &#8220;void&#8221;, see my answer to Question 4.</p>
<p>I hope this helps!</i></p>
		</div>

		<p>I used this code for #1. I think it&#8217;s quite clever.</p>
<p><code><br />
/* This is my answer for quiz problem<br />
 * number one. It takes two values from<br />
 * the user, and adds them.<br />
 */</p>
<p>#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
#include &lt;iostream&gt;</p>
<p>int ReadNumber()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Insert the first number to be added:&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	int add1;<br />
	cin &gt;&gt; add1;<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Now, insert the second number:&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	int add2;<br />
	cin &gt;&gt; add2;<br />
	return add1 + add2;<br />
}</p>
<p>int WriteAnswer(int sum)<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; sum &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	return 0;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;<br />
	WriteAnswer(ReadNumber());<br />
	/* The above code begins<br />
	 * the ReadNumber script,<br />
	 * and once the ReadNumber<br />
	 * script has completed,<br />
	 * sends its return value<br />
	 * to WriteAnswer.<br />
	 */<br />
	return 0;<br />
}<br />
</code></p>

		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-208391">
		<div id="div-comment-208391" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc58df301aaf9474e1d1a1a14608917?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">rameye</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-208391">
		November 3, 2014 at 3:25 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=208391#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-208391", "208391", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to rameye'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>A forward declaration is the function definition without the function body. It tells return type, function name, and the number of and type of the function parameters.</p>
<p>When you call the function after the forward declaration has been given, the number of parameters in the function call has to match with the number of parameters in the forward declaration.</p>
<p>So if you change the forward declaration by making it have one argument instead of zero arguments, then you have to also change the function calls to have one argument. For example from <code>int num = ReadNumber();</code> to <code>int num = ReadNumber(10);</code></p>
<p>If you don&#8217;t do that and still call the function with 0 arguments the compiler will complain about it. Mis-matched parameter list.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251469">
		February 10, 2015 at 7:28 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251469#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251469", "251469", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>It&#8217;s a bit hard to tell because your code didn&#8217;t post correctly, but it looks like:<br />
1) readNumber() is trying to output text AND input values for x and y on the same line.  These need to be separated.<br />
2) readNumber() is using x and y without declaring them.<br />
3) main() isn&#8217;t calling readNumber and writeAnswer() correctly.  Remove the return types.<br />
4) main() isn&#8217;t passing the right number of parameters to writeAnswer().</p>
		</div>

		<p>Huh&#8230; maybe I glossed over it or something, but I didn&apos;t see anything before which said that (x+y) could be used as a single (int x) before for a function. That&apos;s seriously a handy tool to have, though, since I would&apos;ve assumed it to be counted as two separate inputs otherwise.</p>
<p>I can see all sorts of handy uses for such, though&#8230; would clean out a ton of excess functions being used for minimalistic work. I can still see times where you&apos;d want to use a separate add function, or to feed x and y into the function and add them internally, but I can also see a lot of use for being able to essentially skip a step occasionally when it&apos;s not really needed.</p>
<p>My first version fed (int x, int y) and then just had the cout list x+y combined without otherwise adding them except for the output itself. After checking up on the answer after the second test (first one compiled and ran just as intended so didn&apos;t bother to check =P ), I played around with the feeding in of extra stuff as a singular (int x). I may have sidetracked myself from the test with such exploration&#8230; and then started using the if() function to add on more stuff&#8230; you know, there&apos;s a reason I would often run out of time for tests while in school. XD</p>
<p>Oh well, it works, and I learned something new. =P</p>
<p>Thanks again, as usual, for the site and tutorial, awesome as always! Thooouuugh if the combined (x+y) as a singular (int x) thing isn&apos;t mentioned elsewhere though, might be handy to put it in somewhere. That&apos;s assuming I didn&apos;t somehow read over it and just don&apos;t remember. The latter&apos;s probably more likely, but just in case!</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-251617">
		<div id="div-comment-251617" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251617">
		February 23, 2015 at 5:35 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251617#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251617", "251617", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>(x+y) is an expression that is evaluated.  For example, lets say x is 2, and y is 3.  When (x+y) is encountered, the compiler evaluates this to produce a single value (5).  That value can then be assigned (or in this case, passed to a function as a parameter).  You may be right that I never showed an example of this.  I&#8217;ll add one.</p>
<p>Separate function parameters are always separated by a comma.</p>
		</div>

		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-4 clearfix" id="comment-251681">
		<div id="div-comment-251681" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251681">
		March 1, 2015 at 6:11 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251681#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251681", "251681", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>What do you mean it all gives the same answer?</p>
<p>Taking a closer look, you&#8217;re passing two parameters to readNumber: the numbers 1, and 1, which become parameters get1 and get2 respectively.  readNumber prints each of those out individually, then returns the sum to the caller.  That sum is then passed to writeAnswer as parameter total, which prints it.</p>
<p>It&#8217;s weird that your readNumber function adds two numbers together and doesn&#8217;t do any reading.  It&#8217;d make more sense if you renamed it addNumbers or something.</p>
		</div>

Basically, instead of just having it being called twice, I made it so that it&apos;s called once. This kind of shortens what you wrote in the tutorial, but it&apos;s not the same. You can say that I was experimenting around with it to try to understand what is going on.</p>

<p>#include&lt;iostream&gt;<br />
int readNumber()<br />
{&nbsp;&nbsp;<br />
int n;<br />
using namespace std;<br />
cin&gt;&gt;n;<br />
return n;<br />
}<br />
int writeAnswer(int ans)<br />
{<br />
using namespace std;<br />
cout&lt;&lt;&quot;The addition is=&quot;&lt;&lt;ans;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br />
}<br />
int main()<br />
{<br />
using namespace std;<br />
cout&lt;&lt; &quot;Enter the first number :&quot;&lt;&lt;endl;<br />
int x1= readNumber();<br />
cout&lt;&lt; &quot;Enter the second number :&quot;&lt;&lt;endl;<br />
int x2= readNumber();<br />
int ans=x1+x2;<br />
writeAnswer(ans);<br />
return 0;<br />
}<br />
Is it ok Mr. Alex.</p>

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251890#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251890", "251890", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Daniel'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hello Alex,</p>
<p>Just a few questions after having completed the quiz and tried some extra bits to further my understanding.</p>
<p>I have a program that takes 2 inputs from user via one function called twice, it then uses a function to add the two inputs to define a third variable which is then divided using a function &amp; printed using std::cout.</p>
<p>All functions are defined in seperate .cpp files with a header file containing all declarations. (all compiles correctly)</p>
<p>I was just wondering about the header files, does one need a seperate header file for each function e.g add.h, divide.h getInput.h.</p>
<p>It seems it would get really messy quickly having loads of header files for every function. But I can kind of wrap my head around why it may make more sense on huge programs/projects.</p>
<p>Also, on all of my .cpp&#8217; created it says in brackets (global scope), is this correct in the context of these tutorials?</p>
<p>I hope my question makes sense.</p>
<p>kind regards</p>
<p>Daniel</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-251891">
		<div id="div-comment-251891" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251891">
		March 22, 2015 at 9:39 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/112-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251891#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251891", "251891", "respond", "34" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hi Daniel.</p>
<p>You don&#8217;t need a separate header file for each function (as you note, it would get messy quickly).  Typically both code files and their header file counterparts are divided into logical groups.  For example, if you have a bunch of function to deal with vector math, you might have a vector.cpp that defines a bunch of functions, and a vector.h that includes the prototypes for all of the functions in vector.cpp.  That way, if any of your programs need to use the vector functionality, you #include <vector .h> and make sure vector.cpp is compiled into your project, and you&#8217;re good to go.</p>
<p>As for the (global scope), I&#8217;m not sure what that is in reference to.  It doesn&#8217;t sound like anything to be concerned about at this point though.</vector></p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->
